<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer3561938881631109882">In <strong>A Widow for One Year</strong>,<em> </em>we follow Ruth Cole through three of the most pivotal times in her life: from her girlhood on Long Island (in the summer of 1958) through the fall of 1990 (when she is an unmarried woman whose personal life is not nearly as successful as her literary career), and at last in the autumn of 1995, when Ruth is a forty-one-year-old widow and mother (and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3561938881631109882">In <strong>A Widow for One Year</strong>,<em> </em>we follow Ruth Cole through three of the most pivotal times in her life: from her girlhood on Long Island (in the summer of 1958) through the fall of 1990 (when she is an unmarried woman whose personal life is not nearly as successful as her literary career), and at last in the autumn of 1995, when Ruth is a forty-one-year-old widow and mother (and she’s about to fall in love for the first time). Both elegiac and erotic, <strong>A Widow for One Year </strong>is a multilayered love story of astonishing emotional force.<br /><br />This Reader's Circle edition includes a reader's guide at the back. <br /><br />Cover illustration by Honi Werner.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3561938881631109882">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer3022849">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2065699579388597593">I hated this book. John Iriving's inability to write women characters was a huge problem in this book since it has a female protagonist. I didn't care about her at all and I wasn't that intrigued by the story either. I generally like John Irving's writing style, but it didn't make any difference to me with this book because I didn't like one single character.</span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">horny bored housewives</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer8515110">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3656611263974378736">OK here's my final word on John Irving, because I will probably never read anything else he's written (though I've heard The World According to Garp is his best.) His characters are real and they were JUST ENOUGH to keep me going each of the twenty times I nearly stopped reading this novel. The plot is a rambling patchwork in which we never, ever, forget the writer sitting at his typewriter, searching for something to say. When he finds it, he riffs on it till it dies, and then searches for some</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3656611263974378736">OK here's my final word on John Irving, because I will probably never read anything else he's written (though I've heard The World According to Garp is his best.) His characters are real and they were JUST ENOUGH to keep me going each of the twenty times I nearly stopped reading this novel. The plot is a rambling patchwork in which we never, ever, forget the writer sitting at his typewriter, searching for something to say. When he finds it, he riffs on it till it dies, and then searches for something else. I felt sorry for his characters, having to submit to such an unbelievable series of twists and turns.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_10471953">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10471953" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/686677-laura" class="left imgcol" title="Laura"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1197748277p2/686677.jpg" alt="Laura" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/10471953?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 15, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/686677-laura" class="user" title="Laura">Laura</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer10471953">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13049120074843095232">John Irving has yet again created a whole world between the covers of a novel. Characters grow old with the reader, experience lust and loss, love and life. The thoughtfulness of his every detail and the concise placement of every word create a landscape more vivid than reality<br />One of the interesting topics of conversation in A Widow for One Year involves the main character’s attitude towards autobiographical fiction. Irving’s protagonist, world-famous author Ruth Cole, gives one hope that the po</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13049120074843095232">John Irving has yet again created a whole world between the covers of a novel. Characters grow old with the reader, experience lust and loss, love and life. The thoughtfulness of his every detail and the concise placement of every word create a landscape more vivid than reality<br />One of the interesting topics of conversation in A Widow for One Year involves the main character’s attitude towards autobiographical fiction. Irving’s protagonist, world-famous author Ruth Cole, gives one hope that the powers of the imagination can compensate for lacking experience. Or, as would seem the case with my imagination, compensate for inobservance. When Ruth doubts her imagination and seeks real-life research for inspiration, she finds that reality is rarely as scripted and contained as her fiction. Appropriately enough, even her harsh reality is just a construct of Irving (although I am not familiar enough with him to know if it comes from his imagination or not). Ruth vehemently criticizes a journalistic tendency to mirror one’s stories on autobiography; yet one wonders how many of Irving’s repeat themes stick with him due to his personal history.<br /><br />One aspect of A Widow for One Year that struck a chord (other than the glorious title) was the fact that, except for on a few rare occasions, the characters did not change. A love-struck boy, aged into his fifties, carries his idealized boyhood obsession throughout his adulthood. A philandering husband merely morphs into a philandering divorcé, and a woman bearing the sadness of the world carries it with her throughout self-inflicted exile. A whorish best friend does not let life jade her into changing, although she may at times feel a twinge of regret for the innocent pleasures she forfeits. In the end, who has changed? The characters have perhaps learned to understand each other, but doesn’t the understanding they receive allow them to sink ever deeper into the comfort of their own well-established personalities?<br />I also had the pleasure of watching the movie based on the beginning third of this book, entitled A Door in the Floor (starring Kim Basinger and Jeff Bridges). I thought it wise to focus solely on the exposition (if it could even be given such a flippant categorization), which in itself contained more that is real than your typical reader’s life. Although I would eventually become quite fond of our 30-something female protagonist who does not appear until the latter two-thirds, the episodes surrounding her during that fateful childhood summer are certainly what establish the story’s weight and depth of feeling. Ruth’s ability to create such powerful memories of family members that have only ever existed in photographs is a testament to the power of the imagination – both Ruth’s and John Irving’s.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13049120074843095232">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_5826459">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5826459" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/162638-eli" class="left imgcol" title="Eli"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1199836736p2/162638.jpg" alt="Eli" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/5826459?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 07, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/162638-eli" class="user" title="Eli">Eli</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/162638-eli?shelf=novels" class="actionLinkLite">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer5826459">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7663373647290145427">The first thing that struck me about this book was the heart-stopping beauty of Marion, a central character near the beginning of the book. It's tough to get images that concrete in written words, but Irving handles it without strain. Its not just a physical description, its the way that the rest of the image is a bit darker, a bit fuzzier when Marion is in the picture, like Irving is using the depth of field in a photograph to highlight the subject, like her physical brilliance is so overwhelmi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7663373647290145427">The first thing that struck me about this book was the heart-stopping beauty of Marion, a central character near the beginning of the book. It's tough to get images that concrete in written words, but Irving handles it without strain. Its not just a physical description, its the way that the rest of the image is a bit darker, a bit fuzzier when Marion is in the picture, like Irving is using the depth of field in a photograph to highlight the subject, like her physical brilliance is so overwhelming that everything else is dimmed.<br /><br />Looking back, though, that's really all that there was for me in this book. The post-Marion sections of the book are slushy and incongruous; where Irving was so careful not to create a caricature of beauty in the first part of the book, the later parts are almost nothing but caricature: The detective, the prostitute, the distant, womanizing father.<br /><br />In all, this was object lesson in leaving well enough alone. Had the first third of the book been left to stand on its own, it would have been a sparse, elegant novel. With the weight of the rest of the book, it was much less.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7663373647290145427">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_23251914">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23251914" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/184356-erick" class="left imgcol" title="Erick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1443213693p2/184356.jpg" alt="Erick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/23251914?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 03, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/184356-erick" class="user" title="Erick">Erick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer23251914">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1880848926258402036">The first couple hundred pages of the book, before it jumps forward several decades, are the most even, and it is this part of the story that is most endearing. This first part introduces us to the story's three or four main characters and chronicles their shared summer of 1958--a summer which, you guessed it, has profound effects on the rest of all their lives.<br /><br />And it is much of the rest of these lives that Irving takes us through in the remaining four hundred pages, and due to the front-heavy n</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1880848926258402036">The first couple hundred pages of the book, before it jumps forward several decades, are the most even, and it is this part of the story that is most endearing. This first part introduces us to the story's three or four main characters and chronicles their shared summer of 1958--a summer which, you guessed it, has profound effects on the rest of all their lives.<br /><br />And it is much of the rest of these lives that Irving takes us through in the remaining four hundred pages, and due to the front-heavy nature of the book's dramatic set-up it's probably unavoidable that the follow-through seems noticeably lacking in some intensity.<br /><br />But I was still happy to follow the characters along, and for the most part they all seem to end up in their correct places. I didn't buy every plot turn/twist (and the shaky pages set in Amsterdam make it clear that Irving is far more at home in his native New England), but Irving is a great storyteller and easily convinces the reader to weather the impressive storm of plot mechanics he (again) rouses to move things along. All in all: An enjoyable read, but pick up the more-satisfying <em>Prayer for Owen Meany</em> first.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1880848926258402036">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/23160903?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 12, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/849321-jeana" class="user" title="Jeana">Jeana</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/849321-jeana?shelf=good-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">good-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer23160903'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer23160903">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10815316262939189645">I was very close to not finishing this book around page 350 (the first section wasn't so bad, but the middle really lagged). It was very long (very wordy) and to be honest, I didn't care much for Ruth or Eddie. <br /><br />I am glad, however, that I continued reading because it got much better toward the end. I started liking Ruth only when she got married and became a mother. It changed something in her, I suppose. <br /><br />Although this was not one of my favorite books by far, I did like the way John Irving could</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10815316262939189645">I was very close to not finishing this book around page 350 (the first section wasn't so bad, but the middle really lagged). It was very long (very wordy) and to be honest, I didn't care much for Ruth or Eddie. <br /><br />I am glad, however, that I continued reading because it got much better toward the end. I started liking Ruth only when she got married and became a mother. It changed something in her, I suppose. <br /><br />Although this was not one of my favorite books by far, I did like the way John Irving could take something from the beginning of the story--such as Ruth as a child being afraid of a dress moving on a hanger, which I believe came from one of her father's childrens' books, and brought that back when she was waiting in the closet of the red room in Amsterdam. Just having witnessed a murder. His ability to bring back elements like that was nice. And he did it more than once.<br /><br />This book, though, was exhausting in its amount of sex and all things related. And enough about how Ruth had great breasts. I get it. I got it after the first time they mentioned it. <br /><br />Anyway, I don't know that I would recommend this book to anyone I know (in fact, I know I wouldn't) but the last 200 pages redeemed itself for me. But the ending tied itself up a little too neatly for me. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_261384162">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/261384162" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4969354-noce" class="left imgcol" title="Noce"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1302035280p2/4969354.jpg" alt="Noce" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/261384162?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 20, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4969354-noce" class="user" title="Noce">Noce</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/9773166-vedova-per-un-anno" title="Hardcover 9788817862097" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer261384162">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16780907747254655668"><strong>Di come arrivammo al giorno in cui Irving mi portò alle giostre.</strong><br /><br />Inutile negare che con John, io abbia passato innumerevoli momenti felici.<br /><br />La nostra è una storia d’amore ultra decennale. Presi una cotta per lui, quando da adolescente lo incontrai in una libreria di un anonimo paesino di mare, e rimasi ore a sentirlo raccontare le storie di Homer Ne “Le regole della casa del sidro”. <br /><br />Ma poi, come spesso accade nelle storie dei grandi amori, lo persi di vista. Lo rincontrai anni dopo, quando una mia</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16780907747254655668"><strong>Di come arrivammo al giorno in cui Irving mi portò alle giostre.</strong><br /><br />Inutile negare che con John, io abbia passato innumerevoli momenti felici.<br /><br />La nostra è una storia d’amore ultra decennale. Presi una cotta per lui, quando da adolescente lo incontrai in una libreria di un anonimo paesino di mare, e rimasi ore a sentirlo raccontare le storie di Homer Ne “Le regole della casa del sidro”. <br /><br />Ma poi, come spesso accade nelle storie dei grandi amori, lo persi di vista. Lo rincontrai anni dopo, quando una mia amica me lo fece trovare il giorno del mio compleanno con “Preghiera per un amico”, pronto a raccontarmi le storie di una persona a lui carissima, Owen, che presto divenne il mio migliore amico. Ah, quanti pomeriggi insieme a Owen, quante emozioni, quanti pianti e sorrisi. Solo per avermelo fatto conoscere, ancora oggi non ho abbastanza parole per ringraziarlo. <br /><br />Non ci sono mai stati periodi di maretta tra me e John, però come spesso accade alle coppie navigate, ci siamo lasciati prendere la mano dalla routine quotidiana e dalla monotonia dell’abitudine.<br /><br />Ammetto di non essere esente da colpe: anche senza andare da un consulente familiare, sapevo benissimo che ci sono stati lunghi periodi in cui non gli ho dedicato abbastanza tempo. Anni in cui non ho avuto più la pazienza di ascoltarlo, mentre mi raccontava le sue cose. Io ero troppo impegnata a guardare altrove, a cercare persone più affascinanti, che mi sapessero raccontare storie come lui. E ne trovai parecchie, però non è certo una gran scoperta che il primo amore non si scorda mai.<br /><br />Per fortuna, lui si è dimostrato più saggio di me. <br /><br />Così l’anno scorso, mentre ero distratta da mille problemi, mi ha chiesto di accompagnarlo a visitare un altro suo caro amico d’infanzia, Garp. Ed è stata la scintilla che riattizza il focolare. Meraviglioso, come ai tempi di Owen. <br /><br />Così, abbiamo ritrovato finalmente la giusta serenità per dedicarci del tempo, tralasciando le cose secondarie. Una seconda luna di miele. E ho scoperto lati di lui che non conoscevo. Mi ha raccontato ad esempio, di quando era giovane e bizzarro, e scriveva cose che per molti erano incomprensibili, come “Libertà per gli orsi”, e ieri ha voluto portarmi fuori a cena per farmi vedere una cosa.<br /><br />Avevo già intuito che doveva essere una cosa interessante, da quando ho intravisto cosa c’era scritto sull’invito cartonato:“Vedova per un anno”. Un’altra donna si sarebbe spaventata, ma non io, che di John mi fido ciecamente.<br /><br />E ho fatto bene. Pensavo mi portasse a cena in qualche ristorante alla moda, così, per ricordare i vecchi tempi tra luci soffuse e bicchieri di romanticismo. Invece mi ha portato alle giostre. Ma non erano giostre qualsiasi. C’era soltanto un’unica grandiosa ruota panoramica.<br /><br />Per chi non sapesse cos’è, dovrebbe forse essere la giostra più tranquilla tra tutte. Perché come dice Wikipedia è una struttura circolare a cui sono attaccate svariate cabine. La velocità è talmente <em>lumacosa</em> che hai tutto il tempo di goderti il panorama mentre la ruota gira, e ti dà la possibilità di osservarlo per bene da diverse angolazioni. <br /><br />Questo se si trattasse di una ruota normale. Ma potete immaginare la mia sorpresa, quando ho scoperto che la ruota su cui ho fatto un giro con John, poggiava sul perno dell’assenza. Assenza di affetti cari, assenza fisica, e dalle cabine si vedevano tutte le angolazioni possibili di questo vuoto. Un’esperienza paradossalmente <em>piena zeppa</em> di emozioni. E improvvisamente ho capito anche perché il mio amato John mi ci ha portato solo ora.<br /><br />Non è una giostra per fidanzatini nell’estasi dell’innamoramento e della passione. Del resto lui mi aveva già conquistato con Owen quando i tempi erano giusti. Quando aveva bisogno di travolgermi e di togliermi il fiato. Adesso, ha voluto prendermi per mano e farmi guardare il passato. Non il mio, ma quello di altre persone, belle nonostante il dolore, affascinanti nonostante la normalità delle azioni e delle reazioni.<br /><br />È una grande lezione con una tempistica eccezionale. Siamo quello che abbiamo vissuto. Ma in futuro saremo quello che possiamo ancora decidere di essere. Anche se ci vuole un’infinità di tempo per essere padroni di quelle decisioni. A volte ti tocca stare su una ruota panoramica per anni, prima di arrivare ad averne la consapevolezza. Ma il panorama non sarà mai sterile, qualunque cosa ci abbia stravolto la vita.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16780907747254655668">...more</a>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2526930" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/32090-devon" class="left imgcol" title="devon"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1174342489p2/32090.jpg" alt="devon" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/2526930?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 12, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/32090-devon" class="user" title="devon">devon</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer2526930'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer2526930">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14475941214814252365">I don't much know how I feel about this one. The first section of the book is completely brilliant. As is the last line. But in between is rushed, contrite, and full of coincidences that seems like cheap ways to move the story along. Irving gets around conflict in the second part of the book by killing people off. Don't want to deal with the Ruth/Ted conflict? Kill him off! Don't want ruth to have to face her husband about what happened in Amsterdam? Kill him off! And apparently, having a baby s</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14475941214814252365">I don't much know how I feel about this one. The first section of the book is completely brilliant. As is the last line. But in between is rushed, contrite, and full of coincidences that seems like cheap ways to move the story along. Irving gets around conflict in the second part of the book by killing people off. Don't want to deal with the Ruth/Ted conflict? Kill him off! Don't want ruth to have to face her husband about what happened in Amsterdam? Kill him off! And apparently, having a baby solves all internal conflict. (Didn't we learn at the beginning of the book how untrue that is? Shouldn't Graham's resemblance to Timothy have some affect on Ruth?) It's particularly frustrating when a book that takes such care to develop its characters and relationships throws them into over the top plot devices and removes their complexity of emotions. The description of Ruth's feelings about widowhood are quickly jumped over and given half-ass descriptions when contrasted with the loving detail given to Eddie's desire, Marion's sorrow, and even the hilarity of Ted's mistress/gardner scene (which is wickedly funny). I keep trying to justify it by thinking that perhaps the different parts are supposed to be reflective of the novelists in the story, the careful character development of Ruth's style, the crime caper of Marion's style. In the end, though, it feels more like an Eddie O'Hare, deeply and painfully realistic when it sticks to the good stuff, but ultimately fake-feeling, forced, and unsatisfying.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14475941214814252365">...more</a>

Does the author do <em>this</em> in all his <em>books</em>?<br /><br />Omg I just finished it. It sucked so much. The characters were all flat, reduced to one quirk and one obsession, with maybe one exception (Rooie), and OMG again, why would a writer write about a writer writing about a writer? Does the author do <em>this</em> in all his <em>books</em>?<br /><br />Omg I just finished it. It sucked so much. The characters were all flat, reduced to one quirk and one obsession, with maybe one exception (Rooie), and OMG again, why would a writer write about a writer writing about a writer? And what was it with the main character's family of writers, and her mother's lover being a writer too? And why would the author avoid simple names or pronouns, and use "the sixteen-year-old" or "the strawberry-blonde lawyer" or "the prostitute" instead? And the indignation, the tone of narration around "the prostitute" and her colleagues! "The prostitute" was the only interesting character in the whole book, the only one I cared about, and I hated Ruth for what she did (or rather didn't). There. I hated them all. And the stupid dialogue tags, and the italics too.<br /><br />But maybe I'll try other books of this author because I have heard lots of good things about them, and because I am a soft-hearted reader. Really. :)</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_271137884">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271137884" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7011555-bridgette-redman" class="left imgcol" title="Bridgette Redman"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1328158182p2/7011555.jpg" alt="Bridgette Redman" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/271137884?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 01, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7011555-bridgette-redman" class="user" title="Bridgette Redman">Bridgette Redman</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer271137884">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15821263364355222243">I’d forgotten what an intoxicating writer John Irving is. His compelling prose has a clarity and starkness that manages to entertain your brain and soul while permanently incorporating his characters and stories into your memory and being.<br /><br />Irving is not one of those writers who kicks out a new novel every year. His novels are too carefully crafted, too (dare I say it?) literary to be anything less than an evolutionary process. After reading A Widow for One Year, I suspect his books are touchstone</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15821263364355222243">I’d forgotten what an intoxicating writer John Irving is. His compelling prose has a clarity and starkness that manages to entertain your brain and soul while permanently incorporating his characters and stories into your memory and being.<br /><br />Irving is not one of those writers who kicks out a new novel every year. His novels are too carefully crafted, too (dare I say it?) literary to be anything less than an evolutionary process. After reading A Widow for One Year, I suspect his books are touchstones in his life, each representing a period in which he explores an idea or a philosophy. <br /><br />Three in One<br /><br />Irving divides A Widow for One Year into three major sections. Don’t be misled though. None of the three are meant to stand on their own and each would be meaningless without the others.<br /><br />Book one takes place in the Hamptons in 1958. It is in this book that the events which will forever mold all of the characters take place. Irving also includes enough foreshadowing to clue us in on what will take place in the next two books. Somehow though, the spoilers he gives us in the first chapter do nothing but enhance the reading experience and when the events unravel, they still manage to be fresh and surprising.<br /><br />In this first book, Eddie falls irrevocably in love with Marion, Ruth learns to live with death and abandonment, Marion shuts down her heart, and Ted shows himself unable to change despite the traumatic events swirling around him.<br /><br />In book two, Ruth and Eddie meet as adults in 1990. We meet Hannah, Ruth’s best friend and other interesting characters. Irving takes us on a book publicity tour to Amsterdam and forces us to witness that which we would otherwise avoid.<br /><br />Book three takes place five years later (in 1995) and is a book of resolutions. Irving wraps up everyone’s plot lines very neatly. He very nearly gives us a “happily ever after” ending for each person. Happy ending or no, there is definitely an ending with no strings left to unravel.<br /><br />Characters<br /><br />Perhaps one of the most amazing things about Irving’s writing is his intense characterizations. There are no perfect heroes in Widow for One Year and very few villains. Even those people with whom we have the greatest exasperation show themselves in some aspect to be sympathetic. <br /><br />Irving’s characters are filled with quirks. They defy any sort of “norm” or stereotype. Indeed, perhaps some of the strength of Irving’s writing is that just when he’s gotten you to believe that a character is a stereotype, he shows you a different side of them or makes them act in a way that is unexpected, yet consistent with the character. <br /><br />Even the dead have a role in this novel. Marion and Ted’s two sons die four years before the novel begins yet they have a presence that is more than ghostly that permeates every page.<br /><br />Techniques<br /><br />Irving uses foreshadowing better than any other author I’ve ever read does. He tells you in first chapter how the book will end, yet no one will want to leave before he finishes telling the tale. Indeed, you’ll hang on every page to figure out how he will get to the ending he has foretold.<br /><br />Irving also frees his writing from the shackles of chronological time. For all that each book is “set” in a particular year, he freely moves back and forth using both character memories and foreshadowing, making the actual “when” almost irrelevant. A Widow for One Year is a nearly seamless picture of a lifetime. It doesn’t necessarily cover from birth to death, but you do feel you know everything you need to know about each person.<br /><br />One of the real treats in this novel are the stories-within-the story. Nearly all of the main characters are in the publishing industry, primarily as writers. Irving includes their writings as an integral part of the novel. He includes the complete text of two of Ted’s children’s books and summarizes the plots and themes of the novels of Ruth, Eddie, and Marion. We even get a slight peek at Hannah’s writings.<br /><br />A Writer’s Life<br /><br />A Widow for One Year is a book that absolutely resists being summarized in a banal statement such as “This book is about writing.” Or “This book is about sex.” The book is about many things, and is complex enough to have different meanings for different people. However, the complexity of the plot is not reflected in complex writing. It is a very easy book to read and nearly impossible to put down once you’ve started it.<br /><br />Having made that disclaimer, let me say that yet another delightful part of this book is the comments Irving makes on writers and writing. Irving tells us that writers are creative—they create what they write, and yet, even the most original writer draws on his or her experiences and knowledge. I can’t help but wonder whether some of the book tour scenes, interviews, and articles weren’t drawn from his own experience. At the very least, I think he enjoyed poking fun at some of the publishing industry’s foibles.<br /><br />Irving’s books have long ranked as some of my favorite novels. The World According to Garp is a must-read, and A Prayer for Owen Meany affected me like no other fiction book I’d ever read. While A Widow for One Year does not surpass A Prayer for Owen Meany, it comes very close.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15821263364355222243">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_417892209">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/417892209" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2946068-mitch" class="left imgcol" title="Mitch"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1348629583p2/2946068.jpg" alt="Mitch" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/417892209?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 24, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2946068-mitch" class="user" title="Mitch">Mitch</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2946068-mitch?shelf=literary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer417892209">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13367117737428911580">This is one of those 537-page books that, after you've read about 100 pages, you realize you've pretty much seen what the author's handing out and you don't care for it. This is a drag because there is so much more of it to go and only some misguided impulse to finish what you've started goads you stubbornly onward.<br /><br />What's not to like? Well, here's some: <br /><br />At times Irving writes with the grace of a ballet dancer and at others he lumbers along like a blindfolded football player looking for an exit.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13367117737428911580">This is one of those 537-page books that, after you've read about 100 pages, you realize you've pretty much seen what the author's handing out and you don't care for it. This is a drag because there is so much more of it to go and only some misguided impulse to finish what you've started goads you stubbornly onward.<br /><br />What's not to like? Well, here's some: <br /><br />At times Irving writes with the grace of a ballet dancer and at others he lumbers along like a blindfolded football player looking for an exit. The word 'clumsy' comes to mind.<br /><br />I found some of the things his characters said, and many of their actions, unlikely and unbelievable. (His main character chooses to build her workroom in the room where her father committed suicide after a fight with her. She can still smell carbon monoxide fumes (his chosen exit strategy) from the cars below. How...atmospheric. This is a good place to write a novel?)<br /><br />The characters' interactions and lives also seem unreal. The knotted combinations of who is in love or having sex with whom, the main character finding lasting love after a couple of weeks of being with a guy after her continual failures with other men...just no, okay? <br /><br />Also objectionable: the 'comic' parts that deteriorate into vaudeville slapstick...specifically the jilted adulteress gunning for her seducer with her car. <br /><br />One thing that was interesting was a convoluted device that had characters commenting and reflecting on how much or little novel writing contains autobiographical vs. imaginative content. At times this was like looking in a mirror and seeing yourself holding a mirror and looking back. <br /><br />Even so, I will unhesitatingly jilt John himself. I hope he doesn't have a Lincoln!<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13367117737428911580">...more</a>

          </span>

I returned it to the library and am at least taking another break. Maybe I will pick it up in a few months and instead of starting at the beginning again, I will pick up where I left off and continue on. <br /><br />Irving sometimes irritates me and sometimes elates me.   <a href="/user/show/8370501-terri-jacobson" class="left imgcol" title="Terri Jacobson"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1356284278p2/8370501.jpg" alt="Terri Jacobson" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1483599675?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 06, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8370501-terri-jacobson" class="user" title="Terri Jacobson">Terri Jacobson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/8370501-terri-jacobson?shelf=literary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/8370501-terri-jacobson?shelf=2016-bingo-challenge" class="actionLinkLite">2016-bingo-challenge</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1483599675">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5043808579772311127">4.5 stars.<br /><br />I should note at the beginning that John Irving is one of my favorite authors, though I've read only 3 of his books. <em>A Widow for One Year</em> was written in 1998 and is his ninth novel.<br /><br />The protagonist is Ruth Cole. The story starts in the summer of 1958, when Ruth is only 4 years old. The book in many ways is a coming-of-age story about Ruth. But another character, Eddie O'Hare, is almost as important as Ruth. At the onset of the story Eddie is 16 years old and comes to work for the Cole f</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5043808579772311127">4.5 stars.<br /><br />I should note at the beginning that John Irving is one of my favorite authors, though I've read only 3 of his books. <em>A Widow for One Year</em> was written in 1998 and is his ninth novel.<br /><br />The protagonist is Ruth Cole. The story starts in the summer of 1958, when Ruth is only 4 years old. The book in many ways is a coming-of-age story about Ruth. But another character, Eddie O'Hare, is almost as important as Ruth. At the onset of the story Eddie is 16 years old and comes to work for the Cole family. It's almost a coming-of-age book about Eddie as well.<br /><br />The plot is multilayered and rich. (I will not try to summarize it here.) Irving has a knack for truly original characters. The story is told in 3 parts: Summer 1958; Fall 1990; and Fall 1995. Several of the characters are writers, and I very much enjoyed that part of the story.<br /><br />I came to know and love the characters of this book. Irving writes about both family and sexual love. He writes about grief and loss quite intensely. He also writes about adultery, cruelty, and random violence. The themes are well-developed, which is one of the reasons I like this book so much. Irving's prose is a joy to read. He's also a master at inserting comic scenes and characters; I sometimes find myself laughing out loud while I'm reading. And his portrait in this book of an enraged and humiliated woman is unforgettable.<br /><br />A truly excellent reading experience. 4.5 stars.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5043808579772311127">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_74699229">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74699229" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1400289-chana" class="left imgcol" title="Chana"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1373441206p2/1400289.jpg" alt="Chana" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/74699229?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 20, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1400289-chana" class="user" title="Chana">Chana</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=literature-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">literature-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=fiction-family-drama" class="actionLinkLite">fiction-family-drama</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=not-for-kids" class="actionLinkLite">not-for-kids</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer74699229">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10509830779512754800">I had really expected something different. This is the 1st of his books that I have read but I knew he wrote The World According to Garp, Cider House Rules and A Prayer for Owen Meany. Maybe it is just this book but I have to say that Mr. Irving has his mind in the gutter. (sorry to all of you who think this was a terrible thing to say!) He is funny sometimes and he does write memorable scenes, however... right now we are perusing the red light district in Amsterdam. There is sex on just about e</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10509830779512754800">I had really expected something different. This is the 1st of his books that I have read but I knew he wrote The World According to Garp, Cider House Rules and A Prayer for Owen Meany. Maybe it is just this book but I have to say that Mr. Irving has his mind in the gutter. (sorry to all of you who think this was a terrible thing to say!) He is funny sometimes and he does write memorable scenes, however... right now we are perusing the red light district in Amsterdam. There is sex on just about every page of this book and I am sick of it! (more than 1/2 way through the book)<br /><br />OK, I am finished now. He can write a scene (the shredded pornographic drawings in the hedge, the gardner stuck in the hedge - I mean this is memorable writing. He is often funny (his commentary about the use of the semicolon in modern writing had me laughing out loud). The children's stories are terrifying. The Moleman is definitely nightmare material. His writing is compared to Dickens and rightly so, I mean it IS that good. However, and I do mean a BIG however, there are some things I really object to here.<br /><br />#1 A bereaved mother has an affair with a teenage boy. She is susceptible to this because she lost her teenage sons?? As a bereaved mother I find this kind of thinking just incomprehensible. I also didn’t think leaving Ruth was realistic. Most bereaved mothers cling to their surviving children. <br /><br />#2 There is so much sex in this book. It is degrading, and feels insulting. A quote from the book:<br /><br />"But grossness was the norm for many people. Crudeness and prurient interests were the motivating humors for all sorts of individuals;...Whereas she wished more of the population were better educated, she also believed that education was largely wasted on the majority of people she had met."<br />(Mrs. Dash - a character in one of Ruth's stories)<br /><br />another quote:<br /><br />"It galls me that seeking out the seedy, the sordid, the sexual, and the deviant is the expected (if not altogether acceptable) behavior of male writers; it would surely benefit me, as a writer, if I had the courage to seek out more of the seedy, the sordid, the sexual, and the deviant myself."<br />(Ruth, the main female character, a writer) <br /><br />#3 His obsession with breasts makes this story about Ruth's breasts as much as about anything else. Do you think I exaggerate? By the end I think the author must have been joking, the references became so frequent: <br /><br /><br />page 448: <br />"In Ruth's case, you couldn't even see her breasts."<br />page 449:<br />"Yeah, she had nice breasts."<br />page 451:<br />"She had nice breasts, Harry remembered."<br />page 478:<br />"It may have been his anniversary, but he was looking at your breasts."<br />page 482:<br />"And she really did have great breasts."<br />page 486:<br />"Graham won't leave my breasts alone."<br /><br />This is just a tiny sampling of the constant reference to Ruth's breasts. And there are Marion's breasts, Mrs. Havelock's breasts, ... ad naseaum.<br /><br />These aren't the only body parts we hear about, but I've had enough.<br /><br />He also throws the character of Ted away. Ruth has "no feelings" about this. I seem to have more feelings about it than Ruth does! Ruth was the only person Ted ever loved in his life. Was her "no feelings" supposed to be his just reward for how he treated other women? Yuck-pooey to that, and the whole book when it comes right down to it. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10509830779512754800">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_16754456">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16754456" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/842772-kellie" class="left imgcol" title="Kellie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1250816576p2/842772.jpg" alt="Kellie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/16754456?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 09, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/842772-kellie" class="user" title="Kellie">Kellie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/842772-kellie?shelf=2008-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2008-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer16754456">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4399504552951582652">When I think about a book written by John Irving, the picture that comes to mind is a vine; a vine that weaves within itself and spreads thick in it’s own mass. This is the 3rd Irving book I have read. Irving uses some of the same themes in his many novels. Boarding schools, younger men with older women are some that immediately come to mind. This particular book is about a family that lives in The Hamptons. The father, Ted is an author of children’s books. The wife, Marion, is also an author bu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4399504552951582652">When I think about a book written by John Irving, the picture that comes to mind is a vine; a vine that weaves within itself and spreads thick in it’s own mass. This is the 3rd Irving book I have read. Irving uses some of the same themes in his many novels. Boarding schools, younger men with older women are some that immediately come to mind. This particular book is about a family that lives in The Hamptons. The father, Ted is an author of children’s books. The wife, Marion, is also an author but she does not begin her career as a writer until later on in life. The family is ripped apart when a car accident kills their 2 sons. They have a daughter, Ruth, after the accident in an attempt to bring their family back together. When Ruth is 4, Ted hires a student, Eddie, to help him with his writing for the summer. At least, that is what he says. The book is split into 3 parts and covers almost 40 years. Irving takes these 4 characters and begins weaving his plot in such articulate detail, it hovers on the line of genius. After reading these books, I always feel like I missed the true meaning of the story and feel obligated to read it again. I did enjoy this novel, however, I felt overwhelmed on occasion in parts that dragged. I lost patience at times. This still does not take away from my respect for this writer who I think is one of the best in the business. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4399504552951582652">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_432229737">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/432229737" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9077572-irene" class="left imgcol" title="Irene"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1402352450p2/9077572.jpg" alt="Irene" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/432229737?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 11, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9077572-irene" class="user" title="Irene">Irene</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer432229737">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16663591315584265626">This is the only Irving novel I've read so far, but I plan to read more in the future. I read "A widow for one year" about a year ago, and I picked it unknowingly in the bookshop without knowing anything about the author, his style or the story. To be honest, I picked it up just because:<br /><br />a) It was long and it was summer<br />b) It began with the sentence "One night when she was four and sleeping in the bottom bunk of her bunk bed, Ruth Cole woke to the sound of lovemaking -it was coming from her parent</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16663591315584265626">This is the only Irving novel I've read so far, but I plan to read more in the future. I read "A widow for one year" about a year ago, and I picked it unknowingly in the bookshop without knowing anything about the author, his style or the story. To be honest, I picked it up just because:<br /><br />a) It was long and it was summer<br />b) It began with the sentence "One night when she was four and sleeping in the bottom bunk of her bunk bed, Ruth Cole woke to the sound of lovemaking -it was coming from her parents' bedroom".<br /><br />You can beat a beginning like that one, but it's gonna be hard.<br /><br />So I did what I'm not used to do: I entered a literary work without any clues whatsoever. It was great.<br /><br />John Irving is a story-teller, in the widest sense. He takes a character and builds a story around him, normally adding loads of special characters to accompany him. He tells the story with slow, careful writing, paying the right attention to details (the brother's stories and how Ruth memorises the stories through the photographs, just genious) and keeping the reader interested. His stories are long, and he might get a bit tough from time to time, but it's worth it. What I loved the most was how true, how honest, how real his characters were. Neither good nor bad, just people living hard situations and not always taking the right road.<br /><br />I preferred the first part of the book, which focuses on Ruth's parents relationship, rather than the second one (when Ruth is actually old enough to have her own relationships). That might have been because I liked the parents best, and I found both of them appealing and intriguing, I wanted to know what was wrong with them. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16663591315584265626">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_359660953">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/359660953" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/472694-e" class="left imgcol" title="E"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1201447841p2/472694.jpg" alt="E" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/359660953?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 02, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/472694-e" class="user" title="E">E</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/228097.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345434791" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/472694-e?shelf=novels" class="actionLinkLite">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer359660953">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3202750463188369279">The first half is Irving at his best. All those hooks on the wall. All that broken glass and all those windblown sketches. All that honesty amid all that posturing. All that clunky sex amid endless guilt.<br /><br />The second half embodies the detachment, the absurdity, the too-many-ideas-at-once that the main characters have grown to embrace. Perhaps they are defense mechanisms. Perhaps they are the inevitable scars of emotional abuse. Marion is so matter-of-fact about her fate, so resigned to a life that</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3202750463188369279">The first half is Irving at his best. All those hooks on the wall. All that broken glass and all those windblown sketches. All that honesty amid all that posturing. All that clunky sex amid endless guilt.<br /><br />The second half embodies the detachment, the absurdity, the too-many-ideas-at-once that the main characters have grown to embrace. Perhaps they are defense mechanisms. Perhaps they are the inevitable scars of emotional abuse. Marion is so matter-of-fact about her fate, so resigned to a life that will never bring closure, and she drags everyone along with her toward this anti-conclusion. It's unsettling. But what could we expect? Is widowhood, is the death of one's children, is witnessing a murder supposed to be anything <em>besides</em> unsettling?<br /><br />The story is frustrating, but I'm overcome with the sensation that Irving knew exactly what he was doing all along. Frustration is a bold goal to set for oneself, but I can't imagine Irving any other way but bold. It's as ingrained in his writing as the New England landscape. <br /><br />And the novel's jarring last line rivals <em>Angela's Ashes</em> for Most Impressive Last Line Ever. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3202750463188369279">...more</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3938393-jessalyn-hill" class="user" title="Jessalyn Hill">Jessalyn Hill</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3938393-jessalyn-hill?shelf=contemporary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">contemporary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer109195612">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10275585427115226517">This book was slightly disappointing, despite how interesting the plot was. The main character, Ruth, seemed like half a person to me - and what an unlovable person, at that! <br /><br />One interview said the book's "comic masterstroke" was that writer John Irving made all of the characters in this book writers. Ha ha ha ha haaaaaa.....oh wait. That's so funny, I forgot to laugh. It would have been funny if all of the characters were hot dog vendors, for example, or clowns. But I fail to see what is funny</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10275585427115226517">This book was slightly disappointing, despite how interesting the plot was. The main character, Ruth, seemed like half a person to me - and what an unlovable person, at that! <br /><br />One interview said the book's "comic masterstroke" was that writer John Irving made all of the characters in this book writers. Ha ha ha ha haaaaaa.....oh wait. That's so funny, I forgot to laugh. It would have been funny if all of the characters were hot dog vendors, for example, or clowns. But I fail to see what is funny about a writer writing about writers. <br /><br />Which brings me to my next complaint. Most of this book felt like a big inside joke between John Irving and John Irving. As the reader, I felt left out. Then I made the mistake of reading some biographical details about Irving (even though his main character in Widow for One Year, Ruth, says that she never reads biographies of authors because they make her feel let down), and I felt let down. Pretty much everything that happens in the book can be traced back to a true event in Irving's life. John Irving is a very creative person, but I expect slightly more from such a famous author. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10275585427115226517">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a href="/review/list/3714101-karen-klink?shelf=literary" class="actionLinkLite">literary</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer227109958">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17968975947971592661">I started to give this four stars but, the more I thought about it, well, here's five stars. <br /><br />A fifth star (from me) means there has to be something timeless about the story, passages that express moments of what I think of as universal connection and deep understanding. My heart either seems to slow down or speed up and I sometimes close my eyes and feeling a welling up of emotion inside. I suppose if I were willing to spend the time on this review I could express myself better, but here I sit i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17968975947971592661">I started to give this four stars but, the more I thought about it, well, here's five stars. <br /><br />A fifth star (from me) means there has to be something timeless about the story, passages that express moments of what I think of as universal connection and deep understanding. My heart either seems to slow down or speed up and I sometimes close my eyes and feeling a welling up of emotion inside. I suppose if I were willing to spend the time on this review I could express myself better, but here I sit in a coffee shop in Hood River, Oregon, and my husband is waiting for me to get off the computer.<br /><br />Anyway, there were moments like that in this novel. Along with everything else I give four stars for: plot, dialog, sentence structure, intriguing and realistic characters, etc., etc. Plus, if one is a writer, the thoughts in the writer character's head re her development of a novel are especially fascinating.<br /><br />I could not imagine how a sixteen year-old boy would stay in love with a woman he fell in love with when she was thirty-six. John Irving made me believe it, and I was thrilled by the ending.<br /><br />Now I'm off to find another one of his books.</span>

  <a href="/user/show/888138-suzanne" class="left imgcol" title="Suzanne"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1355673827p2/888138.jpg" alt="Suzanne" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14900538?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 08, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/888138-suzanne" class="user" title="Suzanne">Suzanne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/4659.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780345469014" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14900538">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6232938315141406443">One of the great books Irving has ever written, this novel is basically two stories in one. A fairly accurate and well-done film version of the first part came out a few years ago entitled "A Door in the Floor". It was well cast with Jeff Bridges and Kim Basinger and filmed on location on Long Island. <br /> This is the story of a young girl born to two people whose lives were destroyed when their two young sons were killed while driving their parents home after a night of too much drink. The child is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6232938315141406443">One of the great books Irving has ever written, this novel is basically two stories in one. A fairly accurate and well-done film version of the first part came out a few years ago entitled "A Door in the Floor". It was well cast with Jeff Bridges and Kim Basinger and filmed on location on Long Island. <br /> This is the story of a young girl born to two people whose lives were destroyed when their two young sons were killed while driving their parents home after a night of too much drink. The child is conceived after the twins death in an attempt to repair the marriage and remake the "family". It doesn't work. Mom is numb and never bonds with the child and the father, an artist, does bond, but his guilt and his wife's blame and indifference, fuels his need for escape and drink. The life of this girl is the first part of this immense book.<br /> The second part (not in the film) is about her life as an adult: wrong men, wrong choices, guilt, escape and ultimately redemption and happiness.<br /> A very long book. Could have been shorter and the plot turns and twists are often quite distracting. Nonetheless, one of my favorite Irving novels.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6232938315141406443">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Le situazioni proposte e i personaggi sono poco convincenti, anche considerando il fatto che, nei romanzi di questo autore, c’è sempre una componente surreale. Ma, qui, funziona poco anche quella. A dirla sinceramente, mi è quasi parso che Irving abbia un po’ combinato alla bell’e meglio idee sparse e poco coerenti tra loro. I temi a lui cari</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9191361960683435269">Una prova abbastanza modesta da parte di Irving, scrittore che solitamente mi piace. Solo a tratti ho trovato il suo consueto accattivante modo di narrare. Le situazioni proposte e i personaggi sono poco convincenti, anche considerando il fatto che, nei romanzi di questo autore, c’è sempre una componente surreale. Ma, qui, funziona poco anche quella. A dirla sinceramente, mi è quasi parso che Irving abbia un po’ combinato alla bell’e meglio idee sparse e poco coerenti tra loro. I temi a lui cari ci sono sempre, ma appaiono raffazzonati e messi insieme per dovere, più che per la voglia sincera di raccontare.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_151134546">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151134546" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3739451-suzanne" class="left imgcol" title="Suzanne"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1352731541p2/3739451.jpg" alt="Suzanne" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/151134546?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 18, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3739451-suzanne" class="user" title="Suzanne">Suzanne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/587402.A_Widow_for_One_Year" title="Hardcover 9780676970807" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3739451-suzanne?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3739451-suzanne?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer151134546">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16314511663723136222">John Irving is a master at his craft. Therefore, I will never, ever doubt an interest in his books again. <em>A Widow for One Year</em> sat on my shelf for years. I had read two of Irving’s previous books and enjoyed them, but something about this one made me stay away. The premise isn’t something that would normally make me rush out and want to read it. A couple in the 1950?s, lose their two teenage sons in a tragic car accident. They have another child, this time a girl. The mother, Marion, can’t face</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16314511663723136222">John Irving is a master at his craft. Therefore, I will never, ever doubt an interest in his books again. <em>A Widow for One Year</em> sat on my shelf for years. I had read two of Irving’s previous books and enjoyed them, but something about this one made me stay away. The premise isn’t something that would normally make me rush out and want to read it. A couple in the 1950?s, lose their two teenage sons in a tragic car accident. They have another child, this time a girl. The mother, Marion, can’t face being a mother again, and the father, Ted, is a philandering drunk, rather than a supportive husband. Sounds like a book that’s full of psychological headgames. Definitely not for me.<br /><br />And while that is the backdrop, it’s not the story. This tale is about the daughter, Ruth, and while her life is certainly messed-up because of events that happened before she was even born, the events that happen after her birth are fascinating – albeit in a watching a car wreck kind of way. Irving not only weaves a narrative that I found impossible to put down, his writing was a work of art. He understands the power of the written word and how a seemingly simple line can weave a thread throughout a story and take it to a higher place. And I must add this: the last line of <em>A Widow For One Year</em> is the best final line of any book, ever. I’m going to have to put John Irving on my favorite authors list now. 4 1/2 stars.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16314511663723136222">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/317858580?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 23, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8791039-laurent" class="user" title="Laurent">Laurent</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/816626.A_Widow_For_One_Year" title="Paperback 9780552997966" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>
